  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer942978650313895626">Harry White is a man haunted by a satyr's lust and an obsessive need for sin and retribution. The more Harry succeeds -- a good marriage, a good corporate job -- the more desperate he becomes, as a life of petty crime leads to fraud and murder and, eventually, to apocalyptic violence.<br/><br/>Author of the controversial cult classic, <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i>, Hubert Selby began as a w</span>
  <span id="freeText942978650313895626" style="display:none">Harry White is a man haunted by a satyr's lust and an obsessive need for sin and retribution. The more Harry succeeds -- a good marriage, a good corporate job -- the more desperate he becomes, as a life of petty crime leads to fraud and murder and, eventually, to apocalyptic violence.<br /><br />Author of the controversial cult classic, <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i>, Hubert Selby began as a writer of short fiction. He plunges the reader head-first into the densely realized worlds of his protagonists, in which the details of daily life rub shoulders with obsession and madness. Although fundamentally concerned with morality, Selby's own sense of humility prevents him from preaching. He offers instead a passionate empathy with the ordinary dreams and aspirations of his characters, a brilliant ear for the urban vernacular and for the voices of conscience and self-deceit that torment his characters.</span>
  <a data-text-id="942978650313895626" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_572911576" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/572911576" />
    <a title="Lee Foust" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust"><img alt="Lee Foust" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442875445p2/13984676.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/572911576?book_show_action=true">Mar 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lee Foust" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lee Foust" href="/user/show/13984676-lee-foust">Lee Foust</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer572911576" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer963614745568727694">This is a sad day for me indeed. Having finished reading <i>The Demon</i> means that, barring a posthumous collection of some kind--given the appearance of certain non-published texts in spoken word collections not an impossibility perhaps--I have read everything written and published by my second favorite author of all-time, Hubert Selby Jr. <i>The Demon</i> came last in my reading simply because it took me a rather long time to find a copy; but find it I did--last summer at Aardvark Books, my favorite secon</span>
  <span id="freeText963614745568727694" style="display:none">This is a sad day for me indeed. Having finished reading <i>The Demon</i> means that, barring a posthumous collection of some kind--given the appearance of certain non-published texts in spoken word collections not an impossibility perhaps--I have read everything written and published by my second favorite author of all-time, Hubert Selby Jr. <i>The Demon</i> came last in my reading simply because it took me a rather long time to find a copy; but find it I did--last summer at Aardvark Books, my favorite second hand bookshop in San Francisco, CA--and I put off reading it until now mostly because of the bittersweet feeling that I knew would come from arriving at the totality of Cubby's fine pages.<br /><br />Well, personal affections aside, this is Selby's most traditionally plotted, detailed, and therefore realistically-portrayed narrative, and also his most openly Christian text, arriving quite nearly at the parable level. I was a tad surprised and even disappointed at these two features of the novel which otherwise paid off completely in the things that I enjoy most about Selby's work in general, the feeling of one-ness that I feel with the rest of humanity through reading his perspicacious descriptions of human suffering, the great empathy we come to feel for the despicable characters that we otherwise would dismiss and ignore in everyday life, the feeling that I am not alone in all of the mess of being a human being.<br /><br />The reason that I note the heightened realism and more obviously Christian theme of this particular novel is not because I'm against either technique--my third favorite writer is Dante Alighieri, very Christian, and probably next is Ernest Hemingway, master of modernist literary realism--but because I think that these two elements weaken those strengths that I mentioned above, what usually makes Selby's work so uniquely effective at ripping my guts out and making me want to run out and find, shelter, and bed down with the first homeless leper that I can in order to keep them warm and safe all through the night. Really, at his best, no writer gets under your skin like Selby except perhaps for Steinbeck in <i>Of Mice and Men</i> or <i>The Pearl</i>.<br /><br />I was just teaching a short narrative by Italian writer Pier Paolo Pasolini the other day called "Rital and Raton" and in this story the narrator/Pasolini himself apparently declares that to morally judge another human being is to create a power structure, is to lord it over your fellow human beings. As a political anarchist with an a priori bias against power itself--I believe that there is only abuse, never a correct use of power--I objected to the overtly Christianizing of Harry's (Yeah, the protagonists of all of Selby's novels and many of his short stories are named Harry, I love that too) sickness in <i>The Demon</i>. Such a rationalization of his problem tended, in my opinion, to let the reader off the hook, to let the smugly saved look down from a higher position at poor suffering evildoer Harry and his obsession with pity--and that nullified, or at least weakened, for me, the radical empathy that I feel for Harry, Tyrone, Sarah, and Marion of <i>Requiem for a Dream</i> or the man stuck in the cell in <i>The Room</i> or Tralala or any of the many other unforgettable characters of <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i>.<br /><br />Perhaps it's just that I prefer the more overt critique of capitalism/materialaism of <i>Last Exit</i> and <i>Requiem</i> to the more pointedly Christian attack on the sin of pride here, for pride, being personal rather than systemic, makes us readers condemn the character rather that the character's weakness in escaping the sick system surrounding them of the other novels. (For, in this anarchist's opinion, institutional pride is the real sin that creates abusive hierarchies of power: i.e. governments, armies, syndicates, banks, bully systems of all kinds.) It is interesting, however, to see Selby writing about a successful business man here--even with the familiar Brooklyn roots--than the usual lower and lower middle-class tortured souls of his other novels and stories.<br /><br />It occurred to me that this novel was reminiscent of, kind of a modern-day updating of Mathew Lewis' Gothic gem <i>The Monk</i>. Also note that it came about only a year after Blatty's <i>The Exorcist</i> and both novels seem bent on convincing us that evil comes through us from without, from demons. I also just saw P. T. Anderson's recent film <i>The Master</i> and I thought that they shared themes of American alone-ness and the anguish of the search for freedom: to follow no master is to live alone, not to surrender is to become a killer, to lay down with a sandcastle woman, to come to despise others because their physical closeness mocks their actual empathetic distance.<br /><br />FYI: <i>Requiem for a Dream</i>, <i>The Room</i>, and <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i> are all, on a scale of 1-6 stars, eight star novels. Selby's collection of short fiction, <i>Song of the Silent Snow</i> is between 8 and 6, but his last two novels, <i>The Willow Tree</i> and <i>Waiting Period</i>, are, like <i>The Demon</i> a bit weaker. Of them I think I liked <i>The Willow Tree</i> the best although it's still flawed, even overly sentimental. Still, all of Selby's work is so much better than most writers can even dream of being that I recommend them all--but only with your seatbelt fastened for they will take you to places where you do not want to go but you will be better for having gone there and seen those things and wept for those who live there.</span>
  <a data-text-id="963614745568727694" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_453521334" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/453521334" />
    <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls"><img alt="MJ Nicholls" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1374341850p2/2386804.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/453521334?book_show_action=true">Nov 11, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="MJ Nicholls" class="user" itemprop="url" name="MJ Nicholls" href="/user/show/2386804-mj-nicholls">MJ Nicholls</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141195643" href="/book/show/11524282-the-demon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=penguin-classics">penguin-classics</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=merkins">merkins</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2386804-mj-nicholls?shelf=novels">novels</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer453521334" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15739665581002847263">The seventies were seemingly the most productive decade for Hubert Selby, whose short bibliography shows how torturously he composed his tortured (but never torturous) novels and stories. <i>The Room</i> was published in 1971, followed by <i>The Demon</i> in 1976 and <i>Requiem For a Dream</i> only two years(!) later. With his two masterpieces behind him—<i>Requiem</i> and <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i> (1964)—Selby’s work was extremely sporadic and, apparently, subpar. Publication dates suggest this novel occupied him for half a d</span>
  <span id="freeText15739665581002847263" style="display:none">The seventies were seemingly the most productive decade for Hubert Selby, whose short bibliography shows how torturously he composed his tortured (but never torturous) novels and stories. <i>The Room</i> was published in 1971, followed by <i>The Demon</i> in 1976 and <i>Requiem For a Dream</i> only two years(!) later. With his two masterpieces behind him—<i>Requiem</i> and <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i> (1964)—Selby’s work was extremely sporadic and, apparently, subpar. Publication dates suggest this novel occupied him for half a decade, despite <i>Requiem</i> being the superior work, but it’s by no means a patchy effort. <i>The Demon</i> is a “psychological drama” (as TV schedulers say) following the progress of sex-addicted Harry from his womanising years, his attempt to shimmy up the (unconvincing) corporate ladder, to his slow transformation into a serial killer. The prose is typically simple, using Selby’s familiar punctuation style and exhausting run-on sentences. He spends a little too long on the build-up for the climax to have the same devastating wrecking-ball-in-the-guts feeling as his two masterpieces. Arguably, Selby’s depiction of home life is far too cardboard to be wholly satisfying here. His strength as a writer was a profound understanding of what drives people to extremes and the tormented tangles we get ourselves into. (It is hinted that Harry could ‘free’ his demon by simply confessing all his nefarious acts—hmm, probably not, eh Hube?) </span>
  <a data-text-id="15739665581002847263" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1129028875" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1129028875" />
    <a title="Zuky the BookBum" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37830213-zuky-the-bookbum"><img alt="Zuky the BookBum" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1580914187p2/37830213.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1129028875?book_show_action=true">Oct 25, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Zuky the BookBum" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Zuky the BookBum" href="/user/show/37830213-zuky-the-bookbum">Zuky the BookBum</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780141195643" href="/book/show/11524282-the-demon">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37830213-zuky-the-bookbum?shelf=4-stars">4-stars</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37830213-zuky-the-bookbum?shelf=almost-gave-up-on">almost-gave-up-on</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/37830213-zuky-the-bookbum?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1129028875" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16178396674672252364"><i>
  <b>Behind me theres a house, a beautiful house with a loving family, and my gut is filled with rats and maggots that are chewing me up alive.</b>
</i><br/><br/>I feel like the aim of this book wasn’t just to shock, but also as a reminder that wealth, sex, admiration and success don’t necessarily lead to a happy life.<br/><br/>I found, to begin with, this was so great at flowing along with the story. There were no sections that seemed overly long and boring, it was just exactly what we needed to know about Harry’s life. The</span>
  <span id="freeText16178396674672252364" style="display:none"><i>
  <b>Behind me theres a house, a beautiful house with a loving family, and my gut is filled with rats and maggots that are chewing me up alive.</b>
</i><br /><br />I feel like the aim of this book wasn’t just to shock, but also as a reminder that wealth, sex, admiration and success don’t necessarily lead to a happy life.<br /><br />I found, to begin with, this was so great at flowing along with the story. There were no sections that seemed overly long and boring, it was just exactly what we needed to know about Harry’s life. Then we came to after Harry’s marriage, where the demons within him couldn’t be kept down and he was frequently going out and sleeping with random women. Now, I was expecting this to happen in this book, Harry’s known as “Harry the Lover” so it’s pretty obvious that he’s going to be addicted to sex, but it went on for too long. It felt like half the novel was about Harry going out and shagging anything that moved, all the while, his lovely wife was at home looking after their son, and she had no idea what Harry was up to. The thought of my boyfriend / husband cheating on me one of the worst things I can imagine, so having to read so much of it had me seriously wanting to put the book down and move on.<br /><br />Thank God Harry moves on to other things to fulfill his desires, because it definitely helped me get back into reading this. Once I was back into reading this, I was really hooked with the story. I kept wondering how far Harry was going to go to hold down his madness.<br /><br />People get annoyed with the way Selby Jr writes his novels, but I think it’s cool. He doesn’t use speech marks, paragraphs and sentences ramble for ages and he uses a forward slash instead of a apostrophe (it’s closer to type than the apostrophe), this way, he doesn’t ruin his idea and writing flow. I like this raw style of writing, though it can sometimes get confusing to understand who is speaking. Selby Jr is also known for writing about some quite strange, manic and disturbed characters, so his rushed, and a little hectic, writing style does an amazing job as getting you inside the mind of the narrator.<br /><br />This so could have been a 5 star read for me, but I read it during a reading slump (one that I’m kind of still in), plus the topic of infidelity cropped up too much that everything just fell apart and I couldn’t enjoy it as much as I had wanted to. This is definitely <i>not</i> an easy read but also not a read you should pass up if you get your hands on it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="16178396674672252364" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

What you thought was artlessness is calculated manipulation and concrete style. One of the true mavericks of the </span>
  <span id="freeText15081238298141392368" style="display:none">Selby JR lulls you into a false sense of security with his artless, unassuming style. <div id="review_1130600771" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1130600771" />
    <a title="Axel Ainglish" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/37890348-axel-ainglish"><img alt="Axel Ainglish" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1418516641p2/37890348.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1130600771?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Axel Ainglish" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Axel Ainglish" href="/user/show/37890348-axel-ainglish">Axel Ainglish</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1130600771" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5468591385934365418">This is Selby at his best. An outstanding book. An obsessive, powerful one. In which the main character defies himself, constantly, in a sort of permanent bet. Would say is the story of an ordinary, common man, easy to identify with. Till he starts, step after another, becoming something very different. But it all happens as if playing with himself. A quite weird play. That is going to drive him during the whole book. As if he were all of a sudden, lead by some obscure and playful demon. One can</span>
  <span id="freeText5468591385934365418" style="display:none">This is Selby at his best. An outstanding book. An obsessive, powerful one. In which the main character defies himself, constantly, in a sort of permanent bet. Would say is the story of an ordinary, common man, easy to identify with. Till he starts, step after another, becoming something very different. But it all happens as if playing with himself. A quite weird play. That is going to drive him during the whole book. As if he were all of a sudden, lead by some obscure and playful demon. One can share his oddities as a sort of adrenaline shots, of heavier dose each time. No doubt Harry loves risk. Selby's trick is to make us share it. So we see Harry is changing into someone else. Even if he does not really want to. He may not want to do what he is going to. But there is some leading force that dominates him. The risk that it implies, would say. Somehow, he develops a sort of second nature. So, now he has a double life, too. As in paradox, his private one remains a common and good life. Each time even better, as worst becomes his hidden one. He ascends in his professional office life, he marries. And keeps being, apparently, a normal man. But when this happens, he is in reality, something else. Harry has entered in another, cruel, dark, world. Nobody knows who he really is. He does but can not tell. Can not share then, the takeover his inner demon has worked out on him. He has a "man within". And you are already caught in his nightmare.<br />Do not want to reveal the plot, so will leave this here. Take it as a bad joke. Or not a joke at all, as you wish. With a quite unexpected, though logical, ending.<br />Different to his first novel, the hard to read "Last Exit to Brooklyn" with its choir of raw characters and ambience. And far away also from his more experimentally written "Requiem for a Dream", this one maybe his best work. Do we have to say there are no drugs here? . No need of, neither. Will say it has the same, or more catching beat Dostoiesky shown in his novel "The Gambler", e.g. Same kind of evolving, dominant obsession, too. Both authors having been mentioned as owners of a careless style writers, the two of them anyway master what they do. And any comparison to Brett Easton Ellis is, totally out of place, here. For "Psycho" is, in my humble opinion, the book not to be read, of that author. And this one, is an unforgettable work. Also, for other reasons not to be mentioned, Selby has nothing to see with Easton Ellis. Nor with any other, would I say. Hubert Selby Jr. stands alone as the brilliant outsider but classic he already is. And so he shall remain. Do not dare to bet against.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5468591385934365418" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_356834100" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/356834100" />
    <a title="John Molina" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10224492-john-molina"><img alt="John Molina" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1424075707p2/10224492.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/356834100?book_show_action=true">Jun 27, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="John Molina" class="user" itemprop="url" name="John Molina" href="/user/show/10224492-john-molina">John Molina</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer356834100" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9589048841742446209">Wow, that was an intense ending. I've read a couple of Selby's other books (Last Exit to Brooklyn and Requiem for a Dream), and this one was different in that Selby chooses to write about the life of a man who ostensibly has everything he wants in life. In the other books I've read by Selby, Selby writes about drug addicts and thieves and explores desperate characters that an ordinary person would already presume to be a part of degenerative, base activities . What makes this book different from</span>
  <span id="freeText9589048841742446209" style="display:none">Wow, that was an intense ending. I've read a couple of Selby's other books (Last Exit to Brooklyn and Requiem for a Dream), and this one was different in that Selby chooses to write about the life of a man who ostensibly has everything he wants in life. In the other books I've read by Selby, Selby writes about drug addicts and thieves and explores desperate characters that an ordinary person would already presume to be a part of degenerative, base activities . What makes this book different from those is the fact that in this book, Selby explores the mind of Harry White, a successful Wall Street executive. Harry has everything he could want in life: a loving wife, a job that he loves, two young children that adore him, and yet he still finds himself constantly at odds with "The Demon" inside of him. "The Demon" inside of Harry is symbolic of a void that Harry feels in his life; as the book progresses, Harry has to go increasingly desperate measure in order to appease the demon inside of him. Selby does a masterful job of making Harry's descent into madness believable and somehow manages to make the reader feel empathy for Harry, who is basically a horrible human being. Selby is one of the best-- if not the best-- at getting into the heads of his characters. Selby's experiments with style and stream of conscious do wonders in getting into the head of a character, I don't know why more writers haven't tried adapting his style. By the end of the novel, I had a claustrophobic feeling of being inside of Harry's head, and the novel's haunting conclusion left me in awe of Mr. Selby's skills.<br /><br /> Note: I would have given this book five stars, but the middle section was pretty lackluster and having read a couple of Selby's other works(which were masterpieces), I know that Selby could have tightened the middle section up and kept the intensity of the beginning and end of "The Demon" throughout the novel if he had wanted to. I most likely would have given 5 stars if I didn't know Selby's full potential. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9589048841742446209" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer4135849276679406474">Just finished quite a disturbing story of crime and self-punishment. Harry White's tragedy seems to have been inspired by St. Augustine equation of sin with sex. In the beginning, Harry's discreet affairs with random married women feel a bit funny. You wonder what will occur to this man "haunted by a satyr's lust". Then you see Harry getting married and on the fast track to a well-paid corporate job. Yet his obsessiive need for sin and retribution leads him to petty crimes, then to murder and ev</span>
  <span id="freeText4135849276679406474" style="display:none">Just finished quite a disturbing story of crime and self-punishment. Harry White's tragedy seems to have been inspired by St. Augustine equation of sin with sex. In the beginning, Harry's discreet affairs with random married women feel a bit funny. You wonder what will occur to this man "haunted by a satyr's lust". Then you see Harry getting married and on the fast track to a well-paid corporate job. Yet his obsessiive need for sin and retribution leads him to petty crimes, then to murder and eventually to spectacular violence. I wonder, is the moral of the story about the dangers of lust? If it is so, how Catholic is that? I don't think it's an accident that the ultimate outburst of violence is directed at an authority of the Roman Catholic church. I am impressed by Hubert Selby Jr.'s narrative and style. If his writing could be translated to music it would sound as a monumental piece of jazz-rock. </span>

    <a title="Kurt Reichenbaugh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3797062-kurt-reichenbaugh"><img alt="Kurt Reichenbaugh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1391144487p2/3797062.jpg" /></a>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1531108978?book_show_action=true">Jan 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kurt Reichenbaugh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kurt Reichenbaugh" href="/user/show/3797062-kurt-reichenbaugh">Kurt Reichenbaugh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3797062-kurt-reichenbaugh?shelf=beat-other">beat-other</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3797062-kurt-reichenbaugh?shelf=high-brow">high-brow</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1531108978" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8337564233615850972">The Demon by Hubert Selby Jr came along more than 25 years ahead of American Psycho and I wonder if it was an inspiration for Ellis's novel. We have a similar set up, that is a rising young executive with a high paying "job" (it's never made clear what he does at work) who spends many of his hours nurturing depraved obsessions. Ellis's novel took things to the extreme, while in The Demon, our hero Harry dives into his psychosis with a methodical, deliberate approach.<br/><br/>We first meet Harry as an arr</span>
  <span id="freeText8337564233615850972" style="display:none">The Demon by Hubert Selby Jr came along more than 25 years ahead of American Psycho and I wonder if it was an inspiration for Ellis's novel. We have a similar set up, that is a rising young executive with a high paying "job" (it's never made clear what he does at work) who spends many of his hours nurturing depraved obsessions. Ellis's novel took things to the extreme, while in The Demon, our hero Harry dives into his psychosis with a methodical, deliberate approach.<br /><br />We first meet Harry as an arrogant young man with a promising future, and a yen for married women. When they're married, you see, you get none of the commitments, no virginal hangups, no baggage that gets in the way of a young stud having a good time. You score and move on. No tearful goodbyes or messy breakups. You hit it and quit it, and Harry is a master at this game of hunt and conquest. Harry works in Manhattan and lives in Brooklyn with his parents. Between his job, his commute and his home turf there is no shortage of young married women whom Harry can work his charms on. Finding a broad without cashing in on his freedom is Harry's chief goal in life. But, if you've ever read anything by Hubert Selby before, you know that shit is going to go south for our Harry in a bad and ugly way. No one survives Selby's world unscathed.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8337564233615850972" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <span id="freeText11426665923167162665" style="display:none">Hubert Selby Jr. now holds a very special place in my heart; be it a black, dirty place in my heart dripping in blood and other unmentionable secretions that craves stories about seemingly normal people that slowly descend into their own personal hell. Along with the sickly addicting subjects, Selby's writing style is something of it's own. After reading for an extended period of time, you start to feel like you are on some sort of crazy ride that is impossible to jump off of. I finally realized that this "driving" sort of feel to the book is supplied by Selby's lack of punctuation like periods. He is definitely a great American author!</span>

<div id="review_1177565396" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1177565396" />
    <a title="Vit Babenco" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco"><img alt="Vit Babenco" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1365604577p2/19283284.jpg" /></a>

        <a title="Vit Babenco" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Vit Babenco" href="/user/show/19283284-vit-babenco">Vit Babenco</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1177565396" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer916566350702789918">A bit of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6878.John_Updike' title='John Updike' rel='nofollow noopener'>John Updike</a> and a bit of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7464.John_Cheever' title='John Cheever' rel='nofollow noopener'>John Cheever</a> but <i>The Demon</i> is much more morbid and moribund. And it is written in an unimaginative language of an office clerk – <b>Hubert Selby Jr.</b> describes equally routinely both work and love affairs.<br/>“Then the morning and the goddamn feelings of guilt and remorse that churn your swearing body and dull your mind without ever really identifying themselves, the feelings being pushed and shoved desperately down into the cesspool of the gut so they can become confus</span>
  <span id="freeText916566350702789918" style="display:none">A bit of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/6878.John_Updike" title="John Updike" rel="nofollow noopener">John Updike</a> and a bit of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/7464.John_Cheever" title="John Cheever" rel="nofollow noopener">John Cheever</a> but <i>The Demon</i> is much more morbid and moribund. And it is written in an unimaginative language of an office clerk – <b>Hubert Selby Jr.</b> describes equally routinely both work and love affairs.<br />“Then the morning and the goddamn feelings of guilt and remorse that churn your swearing body and dull your mind without ever really identifying themselves, the feelings being pushed and shoved desperately down into the cesspool of the gut so they can become confused and absorbed by something else, anything else, so they do not have to be looked at and recognized and accepted for what they really are.”<br />Somehow I didn’t believe in all those main hero’s sinister psychological metamorphoses at all and <i>The Demon</i> seemed to me to be no more exciting than an amateurish snapshot in a family album.<br />“…and even the ducks in the lake seem to be aware of the solemnity of the occasion…”<br />Really?</span>

<div id="review_740444053" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/740444053" />
    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/24648504-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1560569025p2/24648504.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/740444053?book_show_action=true">Oct 13, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Chris" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Chris" href="/user/show/24648504-chris">Chris</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer740444053" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4588336356351489170"> I read "The Demon" for the first time back in 2011, in one sitting no less. Anyone here read "American Psycho?" Okay, then you have a general idea already of who Harry White is and what he becomes. You can tell from around page 3 where this is going, but "The Demon" is one of those rare books which vindicates itself by making the ride worthwhile. It shows its work. It gives us the unbounded 'yes man;' someone who has been wildly successful in just about everything he has ever attempted, save fo</span>
  <span id="freeText4588336356351489170" style="display:none"> I read "The Demon" for the first time back in 2011, in one sitting no less. Anyone here read "American Psycho?" Okay, then you have a general idea already of who Harry White is and what he becomes. You can tell from around page 3 where this is going, but "The Demon" is one of those rare books which vindicates itself by making the ride worthwhile. It shows its work. It gives us the unbounded 'yes man;' someone who has been wildly successful in just about everything he has ever attempted, save for his relationship with his mother. <br /> Harry White has everything; a lucrative career, money, intelligence, athleticism, loving parents, sexual prowess. But, Harry's internal life is an unrelenting hell of addiction and guilt. He starts out hooked on sex, but as you might suppose, this incrementally progresses. Selby's diction is a broken stream of dialogue which does a brilliant job of conveying Harry's broken, anfractuous thought patterns and self-justifications until you come to see that the man is living a phantom hell under a guise of success. Lots of Selby's work is about love, about the artificial semblances which people substitute for real spiritual fulfillment out of necessity. Harry White is no exception. <br /> What this book does is it hands you the apotheosis of ideals. It says you want it, you got it. Infinite sex? Okay, here you go. No woman can say no to the charming, indefatigable Harry White. Fortune 500 status? All yours. A loving family? Sure, why not? It gives Harry White everything, bar nothing, that modern society (well, modern as of the 1950s) has to offer. But all of these things, to Harry White, are just appurtenances to destruction. They feed his habits, fuel his narcissistic rages, turn him from a generally decent, nice guy into something way worse. <br /> The verdict isn't an indictment of materialism like you'd expect, really. No, the verdict lays the responsibility for Harry White on the shoulders of everyone, no less Harry White himself. There were a million times where someone could have seen him, stopped him, helped him help himself because Harry knew that he was sick and wanted to get better, he just didn't know how. That's where the true tragedy of this book lies. <br /> The prose here is gritty, seedy, very intense. It's not for everyone. But it's also a universal story because everyone has the potential to become a Harry White. This one will haunt you. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4588336356351489170" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_90976051" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/90976051" />
    <a title="J213" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1609660-j213"><img alt="J213" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1407209443p2/1609660.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/90976051?book_show_action=true">Feb 22, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="J213" class="user" itemprop="url" name="J213" href="/user/show/1609660-j213">J213</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1609660-j213?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer90976051" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4265418657620340012">Hubert Selby Jr, most recently known for being the writer of Requiem for a Dream, is becoming one of my favorite authors. Selby was clearly disgusted with humanity and his seething bitterness was brought to life in his novels. Last Exit to Brooklyn was the first book of his I read which hooked me as a fan immediately. Banned in several countries, Last Exit is a brutal and unforgiving take on the realities of a big city and failed attempts at normalcy in a family atmosphere.<br/><br/>The Demon is a perfect</span>
  <span id="freeText4265418657620340012" style="display:none">Hubert Selby Jr, most recently known for being the writer of Requiem for a Dream, is becoming one of my favorite authors. Selby was clearly disgusted with humanity and his seething bitterness was brought to life in his novels. Last Exit to Brooklyn was the first book of his I read which hooked me as a fan immediately. Banned in several countries, Last Exit is a brutal and unforgiving take on the realities of a big city and failed attempts at normalcy in a family atmosphere.<br /><br />The Demon is a perfect example of the old adage, "he has demons," for someone who struggles with inner turmoil and can't be helped. Many authors have attempted the "sane person loses his mind" story, but nothing will compete with The Demon in that regard. I've never been more convinced of a character in a book who just completely loses his mind and Selby's erratic and unconventional writing style builds an atmosphere of panic that will affect the reader. Harry White is an immensely successful businessman in New York City with an amazing wife and family who struggles to control his temptations and the only way to satisfy his demons is to slowly up the ante. Selby never tries to make him a sympathetic or tragic character; to try and make his actions seem reasonable or identifiable. He simply shows you the essence of a complete mental and emotional breakdown that erupts to pure insanity.<br /><br />You can be sure that Hubert Selby Jr's stories will not end on a positive note. There is no light at the end of the tunnel with him. I am in awe at the intensity of his writing.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4265418657620340012" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/101398484?book_show_action=true">May 05, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian Mapp" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian Mapp" href="/user/show/1598886-ian-mapp">Ian Mapp</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer101398484" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3688239693341230739">Third book I've read by Selby Jnr and its easy to see why he is enduring and inspirational. Another excellent, non judgemental review of a seriously flawed character.<br/><br/>Harry White lives as an only child with his devoted parents at the start of the book. He is in his mid 20s and gets his kicks from sex, usually with married wormen, where there are no committments. In one of a number of great set pieces (the greatest coming at the end, with Harrys fall from grace) he abandons his friends in a baseba</span>
  <span id="freeText3688239693341230739" style="display:none">Third book I've read by Selby Jnr and its easy to see why he is enduring and inspirational. Another excellent, non judgemental review of a seriously flawed character.<br /><br />Harry White lives as an only child with his devoted parents at the start of the book. He is in his mid 20s and gets his kicks from sex, usually with married wormen, where there are no committments. In one of a number of great set pieces (the greatest coming at the end, with Harrys fall from grace) he abandons his friends in a baseball game to chase some skirt.<br /><br />Harry is also a successful business man and climbs the slippery slope within his nameless organisation. Taking advice from his boss, he settles down, taking on Linda and bringing a couple of kids into the world. Despite his outward respectability a works event with prostitutes sends him back on the downward spiral.<br /><br />This descent is beutifully written - starting off slow and moving further and further into addiction until he reaches low points - sleeping with alcoholics in very rough surroundings.<br /><br />Cue mental collapse and being reborn - work succcess coming back. However, he looks for other ways to satisfy the itch and starts on petty crime working his way to murder as the thrill wears off.<br /><br />He really is on the way down and the final 30 pages are breathless as he commits his final crime, that links the theme of the book together - sin and redemption.<br /><br />Absolutely wonderful writing - unflinching, non accusing. He tells the stories of working class life, inner doubt better than anyone.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3688239693341230739" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1578040197" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1578040197" />
    <a title="RB" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44768225-rb"><img alt="RB" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1511007787p2/44768225.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1578040197?book_show_action=true">Mar 12, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="RB" class="user" itemprop="url" name="RB" href="/user/show/44768225-rb">RB</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1578040197" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13430233721249148232">After having been floored by Selby's "The Room" a few months ago, I went into "The Demon" not expecting to be as moved and instead I found myself bored, stiff. Our protagonist is another Harry White and he works for a large corporation (and none of this works, not his relationship with the company or anything else with the exception of 3 pages of a sizeable paragraph around page 95) and sleeps with as many women as he can fit into his schedule, loving this little activity so much he even created</span>
  <span id="freeText13430233721249148232" style="display:none">After having been floored by Selby's "The Room" a few months ago, I went into "The Demon" not expecting to be as moved and instead I found myself bored, stiff. Our protagonist is another Harry White and he works for a large corporation (and none of this works, not his relationship with the company or anything else with the exception of 3 pages of a sizeable paragraph around page 95) and sleeps with as many women as he can fit into his schedule, loving this little activity so much he even created his personal game to be played against the women's will. However, as life goes, the story goes and he marries Linda and subsequently she pops out a couple little Whites. The only things that work in this set-up are: Linda's reaction and emotional states with regards to Harry's behaviour and portions of Harry's manic inner monologues where the reader feels as tortured as he is. Sadly, despite some terrific passages and a couple interesting characters, this story never rises to much of anything and ends on a rather dull and predictable note. "The Demon" will likely only be enjoyed by big fans of Selby and should not be the first Selby book one reads, no matter the captions on the front and back of the jacket proclaiming this to be his best - it is his weakest offering.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13430233721249148232" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2261485546" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

<span id="freeTextContainer1293139349170174181">Selby takes on what it means to be consumed by guilt, but lacking the ability to refrain from certain passions. This results, predictably, in heartbreak of the truest kind, the kind you do to yourself. The prose was easy to read and definitely unique to Selby. My only complaint would be the frantic nature of the end of the book, I think it would have dealt a heftier blow if it kept on pace with the rest of the writing.</span>

<div id="review_2935303476" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2935303476" />
    <a title="Annie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/38036772-annie"><img alt="Annie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1586205974p2/38036772.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2935303476?book_show_action=true">Nov 17, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Annie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Annie" href="/user/show/38036772-annie">Annie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/38036772-annie?shelf=read-harder-2020">read-harder-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2935303476" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17976398225194496706">Run away. Seriously, this is not a book for 2020. What a goddamn nightmare of a book (as all Selby's books are, but man this one feels more personal or intimate or something).<br/><br/>Ok, let me give you the premise: a young, successful young man named Harry slowly starts to lose his mind, drive by a truly insane need for adrenaline. That's it, that's the book. <br/><br/>Except... shit is <i>unhinged.</i><br/><br/><a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[In the beginning, he satisfies his need for adrenaline by sleeping with married women. Pretty harmless.</span></span>
  <span id="freeText17976398225194496706" style="display:none">Run away. Seriously, this is not a book for 2020. What a goddamn nightmare of a book (as all Selby's books are, but man this one feels more personal or intimate or something).<br /><br />Ok, let me give you the premise: a young, successful young man named Harry slowly starts to lose his mind, drive by a truly insane need for adrenaline. That's it, that's the book. <br /><br />Except... shit is <i>unhinged.</i><br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[In the beginning, he satisfies his need for adrenaline by sleeping with married women. Pretty harmless. They're consenting, they're delighted to be seduced by this young, attractive young man who actually listens to them and pleases them, unlike their preoccupied husbands. And the fear of possibly getting discovered by their husbands keeps him happy. <br /><br />Then he catches feelings for a coworker, Linda (who he calls "the Lady of the Laugh"). They get married, have a son. He truly does love her--very deeply--but before long the enthusiasm of the new love and new family fades, and he finds himself obsessed with finding a new outlet. He starts cheating on his wife-- with a coworker at first, and then (out of fear she'd find out if he continued to sleep with people they know) with sex workers. First, high end sex workers. And then, lower class sex workers. He is panicked by his own actions-- what if he were to pass an STD onto Linda?--so he stops having sex with her. Linda is desperate, has no idea why her wonderful husband is so withdrawn emotionally and physically. <br /><br />Finally, he manages to suppress the drive for a while. Gets into plant collecting. Every time he needs a kick, he buys a new houseplant and it gives him a nice dopamine jolt. Their house becomes a jungle (sadly, I can relate). <br /><br />But after a while, that's no longer sustaining him, either. The houseplants wither and die, an outward expression of the death of his marriage and his sanity. The energy of suppressing this desire for constant risk and a rush consumes him. He stops going to his hard-won job (VP of a bank). Takes a leave of absence. Starts having random screaming outbursts in public. <br /><br />Unable to suppress it any longer, he takes up stealing. Small things. Change, a meal, a typewriter. It's comparatively safe, a controlled risk. But it, too, loses its ability to satisfy him before long. It's too easy. So he starts jumping in front of trains, leaping across the tracks right before the engine. That's great fun, but before long (of course) it stops carrying the heart-pounding thrill he so badly craves. <br /><br />Finally, the thought occurs to him-- a thought he supposes has been in the back of his mind all along, a necessary conclusion, an inevitability: he's going to have to kill someone next. <br /><br />And for a long time-- months, and months, and months-- just the thought of it is enough to give him a thrill. He makes no plans. It's just a secret he can return to when he needs the rush. The knowledge. Someday. Someday. <br /><br />Eventually, of course, that's not enough, either. So he finally does it one day. In a subway station, he pushes a stranger in front of a moving train. The man is shredded.<br /><br />That memory feeds him for a while, but before long (duh, you know how this works now) it's not enough. He has to do it again-- more intimately this time. <br /><br />So he stabs in a stranger in a crowd next. Then he disappears from his family's home, unable to bear the idea of sitting down with his wife and son after what he's done (see, Harry isn't a sociopath, he's really fucked up by what he's done, but he truly just cannot control the impulses, which is the most unsettling part of the book-- he's relatable, he's not a Hannibal Lecter). He wanders around the shoreline for a while, barefoot and without a coat like the madman he is.<br /><br />At last, he wanders into a church in Manhattan, where he stabs the cardinal of New York in the middle of Mass. And kills himself. Which, let's be honest, he probably should have done before he started killing other people.  <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />Honestly, as fucked up as it is, I liked this one a lot. It was perfectly done, the slow descent into madness. Every step seemed completely believable because of the delicateness with which Selby set it up. Also, weirdly, Harry is easily one of the more appealing of Selby's protagonists. To me, anyway. Not sure what that says about my brain. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17976398225194496706" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14526079078396649095">This book was all too familiar. Quite similar to <i>American Psycho</i> by Bret Easton Ellis, with the exception of that was a far better book than this one. Far better. I'm really not understanding all of the 4 and 5 stars this book has received. <br/><br/>Once you get past the lack of quotations, apostrophes, and continuous, ongoing sentences during the "what's-going-on-in-my-head" scenes, you're able to focus more on the plot.   <span id="freeText14526079078396649095" style="display:none">This book was all too familiar. Quite similar to <i>American Psycho</i> by Bret Easton Ellis, with the exception of that was a far better book than this one. Far better. I'm really not understanding all of the 4 and 5 stars this book has received. <br /><br />Once you get past the lack of quotations, apostrophes, and continuous, ongoing sentences during the "what's-going-on-in-my-head" scenes, you're able to focus more on the plot. This plot isn't nearly as graphic as the aforementioned book, unfortunately. The build up starts. Things get worse as expected. For me, though, the build up leads to a very unrealistic unfolding of events. Just not believable. So that was the let down for me. <br /><br />The grammar issues, the lack of gore considering the plot, and the let down led me to knock off 2 stars. Thinking of knocking off another just cuz I'm annoyed.</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer8955398127354376814">This one was hard to put down at the beginning. The progtagonist, Harry White, is a real bastard. But he's unaware of that, and finds interesting ways to justify his behavior. Stangely, he's also an optimist, at least initially, a deluded contrast I found fascinating. <br/><br/>The humor is subtle, and dark, my favorite kind.<br/><br/>Styleistically, the book is intriguing as well: Selby doesn't attribute in direct dialogue, doesn't use quote marks, and uses some other odd techniques that I hadn't seen before, yet </span>
  <span id="freeText8955398127354376814" style="display:none">This one was hard to put down at the beginning. The progtagonist, Harry White, is a real bastard. But he's unaware of that, and finds interesting ways to justify his behavior. Stangely, he's also an optimist, at least initially, a deluded contrast I found fascinating. <br /><br />The humor is subtle, and dark, my favorite kind.<br /><br />Styleistically, the book is intriguing as well: Selby doesn't attribute in direct dialogue, doesn't use quote marks, and uses some other odd techniques that I hadn't seen before, yet it all flowed clearly.<br /><br />The book also ended strongly. <br /><br />A criticism would be that the middle seemed padded and is a bit monotonous. It seems like the book could have 100 pages cut from the middle and not lose anything.<br /><br />Still, I will be reading more Selby.</span>

//<![CDATA[  

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('iAgE/lz3CQ7AW44a9k4ScXr4oFDJu3vK/9oNYJB97PCyLm0s56nAZmdwOnsBuxiurKdHmAc29a2kpEvRXT4eag==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('JJTphepaevt3npduevo7kabPr5i+MQyRGERloH+JURkesoBXUQSzk9C1Iw+NDzFOcJBIUHC8gvZDOiMRssqjgw==')})
  }

      <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/26335.Hubert_Selby_Jr_">About Hubert Selby Jr.</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/26335.Hubert_Selby_Jr_"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1397502493p3/26335.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/26335.Hubert_Selby_Jr_">Hubert Selby Jr.
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
1,963
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer5976666563665052869">Hubert Selby, Jr. was born in Brooklyn and went to sea as a merchant marine while still in his teens. Laid low by lung disease, he was, after a decade of hospitalizations, written off as a goner and sent home to die. Deciding instead to live, but having no way to make a living, he came to a realization that would change the course of literature: "I knew the alphabet. Maybe I could be a writer." Dr</span>
  <span id="freeText5976666563665052869" style="display:none">Hubert Selby, Jr. was born in Brooklyn and went to sea as a merchant marine while still in his teens. Laid low by lung disease, he was, after a decade of hospitalizations, written off as a goner and sent home to die. Deciding instead to live, but having no way to make a living, he came to a realization that would change the course of literature: "I knew the alphabet. Maybe I could be a writer." Drawing from the soul of his Brooklyn neighborhood, he began writing something called "The Queen Is Dead," which evolved, after six years, into his first novel, <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn</i> (1964), a book that Allen Ginsberg predicted would "explode like a rusty hellish bombshell over America and still be eagerly read in a hundred years."<br /><br />Selby's second novel, <i>The Room</i> (1971), considered by some to be his masterpiece, received, as Selby said, "the greatest reviews I've ever read in my life," then rapidly vanished leaving barely a trace of its existence. Over the years, however, especially in Europe, <i>The Room</i> has come to be recognized as what Selby himself perceives it to be: the most disturbing book ever written, a book that he himself was unable to read again for twenty years after writing it.<br /><br />"A man obsessed / is a man possessed / by a demon." Thus the defining epigraph of <i>The Demon</i> (1976), a novel that, like <i>The Room</i>, has been better understood and more widely embraced abroad than at home.<br /><br />If <i>The Room</i> is Selby's own favorite among his books, <i>Requiem for a Dream</i> (1978) contains his favorite opening line: "Harry locked his mother in the closet." It is perhaps the truest and most horrific tale of heroin addiction ever written. <br /><br /><i>Song of the Silent Snow</i> (1986) brought together fifteen stories whose writing spanned more than twenty years.<br /><br />Selby continued to write short fiction, screenplays and teleplays at his apartment in West Hollywood. His work appeared in many journals, including <i>Yugen, Black Mountain Review, Evergreen Review, Provincetown Review, Kulchur, New Directions Annual, Swank</i> and <i>Open City</i>. For the last 20 years of his life, Selby taught creative writing as an adjunct professor in the Master of Professional Writing program at the University of Southern California. Selby often wryly noted that The New York Times would not review his books when they were published, but he predicted that they'd print his obituary.<br /><br />The movie <i>Last Exit to Brooklyn,</i> Directed by Uli Edel, was made in 1989 and his 1978 novel <i>Requiem for a Dream</i> was made into a film that was released in 2000. Selby himself had a small role as a prison guard. <br /><br />In the 1980s, Selby made the acquaintance of rock singer Henry Rollins, who had long admired Selby's works and publicly championed them. Rollins not only helped broaden Selby's readership, but also arranged recording sessions and reading tours for Selby. Rollins issued original recordings through his own 2.13.61 publications, and distributed Selby's other works.<br /><br />During the last years of his life, Selby suffered from depression and fits of rage, but was always a caring father and grandfather. The last month of his life Selby spent in and out of the hospital. He died in Highland Park, Los Angeles, California of chronic obstructive pulmonary lung disease. Selby was survived by his wife of 35 years, Suzanne; four children and 11 grandchildren.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5976666563665052869" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

</div>
